<p><b>Inca mythology</b> of the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> was based on pre-Inca beliefs that can be found in the <a href="/wiki/Huarochir%C3%AD_Manuscript" title="Huarochirí Manuscript">Huarochirí Manuscript</a>, and in pre-Inca cultures including <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paracas_culture" title="Paracas culture">Paracas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">Moche</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nazca_culture" title="Nazca culture">Nazca culture</a>. The mythology informed and supported <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Inca_Empire" title="Religion in the Inca Empire">Inca religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>One of the most important figures in pre-Inca Andean beliefs was the <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator deity</a> <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a>.  During Inca times, Viracocha remained significant - he was seen as the creator of all things, or the substance from which all things are created, and intimately associated with the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Dover_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dover-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to legend, the founder of the Inca Dynasty in Peru and the Cusco Dynasty at <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a> was <a href="/wiki/Manco_C%C3%A1pac" title="Manco Cápac">Manco Cápac</a>. His history is unclear, especially concerning his rule at Cuzco and his origins. In one story, he was the son of Viracocha. In another, he was raised from the depths of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a> by the sun god <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>. Commoners were not allowed to speak the name of Viracocha, which is possibly an explanation for the need for three foundation legends rather than just one.<sup id="cite_ref-History_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Inca cosmology was ordered in three spatio-temporal levels or <i><a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)#Ukhu_Pacha" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">Pachas</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heydt-Coca_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heydt-Coca-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These included: <a href="/wiki/Uku_Pacha" class="mw-redirect" title="Uku Pacha">Uku Pacha</a> ("the lower world"), which was located within the earth's surface; <a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)#Kay_Pacha" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">Kay Pacha</a>, which was the material world;  and <a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)#Hanan_Pacha" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">Hanan Pacha</a> ("higher world"), which was the world above us where the sun and moon lived.<sup id="cite_ref-Steele2004_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steele2004-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Inca society was influenced by the local animal populations; both as food, textile, and transport sources, as well as religious and cultural cornerstones. Many myths and legends of the Inca include or are solely about an animal or a mix of animals and their interactions with the gods, humans, and or natural surroundings. Animals were also important in Incan astronomy, with the Milky Way symbolized as a river, with the stars within it being symbolized as animals that the Inca were familiar with in and around this river.<sup id="cite_ref-Gullberg_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gullberg-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Pre-Inca_Andean_beliefs">Pre-Inca Andean beliefs</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Pre-Inca Andean beliefs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Pre-Inca beliefs can be found in the <a href="/wiki/Huarochir%C3%AD_Manuscript" title="Huarochirí Manuscript">Huarochirí Manuscript</a>, a 17th-century text that records the myths, culture, and beliefs of people in the <a href="/wiki/Huarochir%C3%AD_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Huarochirí Province">Huarochirí Province</a> of the Western Andes.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other pre-Inca cultures include <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paracas_culture" title="Paracas culture">Paracas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">Moche</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nazca_culture" title="Nazca culture">Nazca culture</a>. 
</p><p>One of the most important figures in pre-Inca Andean beliefs is the <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator deity</a> <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a>, who even during Inca times was one of the most important deities in the Inca pantheon and seen as the creator of all things, or the substance from which all things are created, and intimately associated with the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Dover_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dover-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In pre-Incan Andean <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> Viracocha takes the form of a <a href="/wiki/Staff_God" title="Staff God">Staff God</a>, characterized by front-facing figures holding vertical objects which are referred two as "staffs". As the chief deity, Viracocha was the creator god and served as the primary religious icon of the entire Peruvian Andes, particularly during the Early Horizon (900-200 BC) onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Basic_beliefs">Basic beliefs</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Basic beliefs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1251242444">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .ambox{display:none!important}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Inca_mythology" title="Special:EditPage/Inca mythology">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2024</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scholarly research demonstrates that Runa (<a href="/wiki/Quechuan_languages" title="Quechuan languages">Quechua</a> speakers) belief systems were integrated with their view of the cosmos, especially in regard to the way that the Runa observed the motions of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> and the solar system as seen from <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a>, the capital of Tawantinsuyu whose name means "rock of the owl". From this perspective, their stories depict the movements of constellations, planets, and planetary formations, which are all connected to their agricultural cycles. This was especially important for the Runa, as they relied on cyclical agricultural seasons, which were not only connected to annual cycles, but to a much wider cycle of time (every 800 years at a time). This way of keeping time was deployed in order to ensure the cultural transmission of key information, in spite of regime change or social catastrophes.
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire">Spanish conquest of Peru</a> by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Pizarro" title="Francisco Pizarro">Francisco Pizarro</a>, colonial officials burned the records kept by the Runa.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> There is currently a theory put forward by <a href="/wiki/Gary_Urton" title="Gary Urton">Gary Urton</a> that the <a href="/wiki/Quipu" title="Quipu">quipus</a> could have been a <a href="/wiki/Binary_numeral_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Binary numeral system">binary</a> system capable of recording <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonological</a> or <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logographic</a> <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a>. Still, to date, all that is known is based on what was recorded by priests, from the <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> on Inca pottery and architecture, and from the myths and legends that have survived among the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a> of the Andes.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Inca_foundation_legends">Inca foundation legends</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Inca foundation legends"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Manco_C%C3%A1pac" title="Manco Cápac">Manco Cápac</a> was the legendary founder of the Inca Dynasty in Peru and the Cusco Dynasty at <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a>. The legends and history surrounding him are very contradictory, especially those concerning his rule at Cuzco and his origins. In one legend, he was the son of <a href="/wiki/Inca_Viracocha" class="mw-redirect" title="Inca Viracocha">Viracocha</a>. In another, he was brought up from the depths of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a> by the sun god <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>. However, commoners were not allowed to speak the name of Viracocha, which is possibly an explanation for the need for three foundation legends rather than just one.<sup id="cite_ref-History_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There were also many myths about Manco Cápac and his coming to power. In one myth, Manco Cápac and his brother <a href="/wiki/Pacha_Kamaq" title="Pacha Kamaq">Pacha Kamaq</a> were sons of the sun god <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>. Manco Cápac was worshiped as the fire and sun god. In another myth, Manco Cápac was sent with Mama Ocllo (others even mention numerous siblings) to Lake Titicaca where they resurfaced and settled on the Isla Del Sol. According to this legend, Manco Cápac and his siblings were sent up to the earth by the sun god and emerged from the cave of Puma Orco at <a href="/wiki/Paqariq_Tampu" title="Paqariq Tampu">Paqariq Tampu</a> carrying a golden staff called "tapac-yauri". They were instructed to create a Temple of the Sun in the spot where the staff sank into the earth to honor the sun god Inti, their father. During the journey, one of Manco's brothers (<a href="/wiki/Ayar_Cachi" title="Ayar Cachi">Ayar Cachi</a>) was tricked into returning to Puma Urqu and sealed inside or alternatively was turned to ice, because his reckless and cruel behavior angered the tribes that they were attempting to rule. (<i><a href="/wiki/Huaca" title="Huaca">huaca</a></i>).
</p><p>Pedro Sarmiento de Gamboa wrote that there was a hill referred to as Tambotoco, about 33 kilometers from Cuzco, where eight men and women emerged as the original Inca's. The men were <a href="/wiki/Manco_C%C3%A1pac" title="Manco Cápac">Manco Capac</a>, Ayar Auca, <a href="/wiki/Ayar_Cachi" title="Ayar Cachi">Ayar Cachi</a>, and Ayar Uchu. The women were <a href="/wiki/Mama_Ocllo" title="Mama Ocllo">Mama Ocllo</a>, Mama Huaco, Mama Ipacura, and Mama Raua.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In another version of this legend, instead of emerging from a cave in Cuzco, the siblings emerged from the waters of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a>. Since this was a later origin myth than that of Pacaritambo it may have been created as a ploy to bring the powerful Aymara tribes into the fold of the Tawantinsuyo.
</p><p>In the Inca Virachocha legend, Manco Cápac was the son of <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Inca Viracocha</a> of Paqariq Tampu which is 25&#160;km (16&#160;mi) south of Cuzco. He and his brothers (Ayar Auca, <a href="/wiki/Ayar_Cachi" title="Ayar Cachi">Ayar Cachi</a>, and Ayar Uchu); and sisters (<a href="/wiki/Mama_Ocllo" title="Mama Ocllo">Mama Ocllo</a>, Mama Huaco, Mama Raua, and <a href="/wiki/Mama_Cura" class="mw-redirect" title="Mama Cura">Mama Cura</a>) lived near <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a> at Paqariq Tampu, and uniting their people and the ten <a href="/wiki/Ayllu" title="Ayllu">ayllu</a> they encountered in their travels to conquer the tribes of the Cusco Valley. This legend also incorporates the golden staff, which is thought to have been given to Manco Cápac by his father. Accounts vary, but according to some versions of the legend, the young Manco jealously betrayed his older brothers, killed them, and then became Cusco.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Deities">Deities</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Deities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Diablo_pune%C3%B1o.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Diablo_pune%C3%B1o.jpg/200px-Diablo_pune%C3%B1o.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Diablo_pune%C3%B1o.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="230" data-file-height="287" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Supay" title="Supay">Supay</a>, god of death, as interpreted in a carnival festival </figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1251242444"><table class="box-Prose plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is in <a href="/wiki/MOS:LIST" class="mw-redirect" title="MOS:LIST">list</a> format but may read better as <a href="/wiki/MOS:PROSE" class="mw-redirect" title="MOS:PROSE">prose</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit">converting this section</a>, if appropriate. <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">Editing help</a> is available.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2024</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1251242444"><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Inca_mythology" title="Special:EditPage/Inca mythology">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22">"Inca mythology"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Inca+mythology%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Inca+mythology%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2024</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Incas permitted the cultures they integrated into their empire to keep their individual religions. Some of the various gods worshiped by the peoples of the Incan empire had overlapping responsibilities and domains. These were worshipped by different <a href="/wiki/Ayllu" title="Ayllu">ayllus</a> or worshipped in particular former states.<sup id="cite_ref-Steele2008_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steele2008-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Apu_(god)" title="Apu (god)">Apu</a></i> (<i>Great lord</i>) was a god or spirit of mountains. All of the important mountains have their own Apu, and some of them receive sacrifices to bring out certain aspects of their being. Some rocks and caves also are credited as having their own apu.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Amaru_(mythology)" title="Amaru (mythology)">Amaru</a></i> (<i>Sacred serpent</i>) was a <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">serpent</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragon</a> deity often represented as a winged serpent, with crystalline eyes, a reddish snout, a <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> head, <a href="/wiki/Taruca" title="Taruca">taruka</a> horns, and a fish tail, depending on the variations of the Amaru, whether in the various animal features, names or tonality of its skin according to the legend told, the ophidic form of the Amaru was always present. In the Inca mythology, it was a symbol of <a href="/wiki/Wisdom" title="Wisdom">wisdom</a>, which is why the image of said totemic being was placed in the children of the Houses of Knowledge "Yachay Wasikuna". Amaru is associated with the economy of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, that irrigate the agricultural lands, symbolizing the vitality of the water that allows the existence of the Aymara people. Thus the deity Amaru symbolizes the water that runs through the irrigation canals, rivers and springs and that makes it possible for the seeds of the <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">crop</a> to be transformed into vegetables. Amaru is a mythical being that is also related to the underworld, the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a>. Despite the fact of Amaru dragons being benevolent deities, some Amarus have violent behaviour against humans. There's a myth called "Amaru Aranway" that is about two Amarus fighting against each other, causing destruction and death as the fight still goes on, then Viracocha send the god Illapa (Thunder) and Wayrapuka (Wind) to defeat them, the two Amarus tried to fight the gods but then they tried to escape flying to the skies, but Wayrapuka drag them back to earth with the power of wind and Illapa fought and put the final blow to them, when the two Amarus died, they turned into the chain of mountains that are located in <a href="/wiki/Mantaro_Valley" title="Mantaro Valley">valle del Mantaro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>.</li>
<li><i>Ataguchu</i> (a.k.a. <i>Atagujo, Ataguju</i>) was a god who assisted in <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">creation myth</a>. The legend says Ataguchu, tired due of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">Cosmos</a> loneliness, created some divine beings to be his servants, at the same time, he created Guamansuri and sent him to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">earth</a> (more precisely, the Huamanchuco province). The province was inhabited by an ethnic group called Guachemines, Guamansuri as a foreigner, was at service of the group, Guachemines leaders had a sister called Cautaguan, due to her beautifulness, she was confined. However, one day Guamansuri seduced and got Cautaguan pregnant, when the leaders realized her sister was pregnant, they instantly knew Guamansuri was the culprit, so they captured and burned Guamansuri, then they scattered his ashes. Guamansuri ashes went up to the <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a> and stayed there with Ataguchu. The leaders put Cautaguan under strict surveillance and, in a few days, she gave birth to two eggs, she dying in childbirth. They took the eggs and put them in a dunghill where two screaming children came out of them. A servant took care of the children, they named them Catequil and Piguerao (in one version, it's stated that Piguerao was born with a weak condition and hence he died shortly after. In other version, he supports his brother in order to defeat the Guachemines). Catequil went to where his mother had died and brought her back to life. She gave him the <a href="/wiki/Sling_(weapon)" title="Sling (weapon)">slings</a> that Guamansuri had left for him, so that he could kill the Guachemines. Catequil killed many Guachemines and expelled those he did not kill from the country. So he went to <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">heaven</a> and informed Ataguchu that the land was already free of the Guachemines and asked him to create the people to inhabit and work it. Ataguchu told him to go to the hill and to the high pasture lands called Guacat, upstream from the actual city of La Parilla de Santa. Once there, both brothers pulled the Indians out of the land using gold and silver tools.</li>
<li><i>Auquis</i> were deities that watched over each populated region.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Axomamma" title="Axomamma">Axomamma</a></i> (<i>Mother of potatoes</i>) was a goddess of <a href="/wiki/Solanum_tuberosum" class="mw-redirect" title="Solanum tuberosum">potatoes</a>.</li>
<li><i>Catequil</i> (A.k.a. <i>Apocatequil, Apu Catequil</i>) was the tutelar god of <a href="/wiki/Day" title="Day">day</a> and <a href="/wiki/Good" title="Good">good</a>, he's also the god of <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> in northern Peruvian highlands. Catequil and his twin brother Piguerao were born from hatched eggs. It's believed that Catequil was another name or representation of god Illapa.</li>
<li><i>Cavillace</i> was a virgin goddess who ate a fruit, which was actually the sperm of Coniraya, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> god and mother of the <a href="/wiki/Qoya" class="mw-redirect" title="Qoya">Coya</a>, who raised the <a href="/wiki/%C3%91usta" title="Ñusta">ñusta</a> of the empire. When she gave birth to a son, she demanded that the father step forward. No one did, so she put the baby on the ground and it crawled towards Coniraya. She was ashamed because of Coniraya's low stature among the gods, and ran to the coast of Peru, where she changed herself and her son into rocks.</li>
<li><i>Ch'aska</i> (<i>Morning star</i>; a.k.a. <i>Ch'aska Quyllur</i>) was the goddess of <a href="/wiki/Dawn" title="Dawn">dawn</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dusk" title="Dusk">dusk</a>, as well as the goddess of <a href="/wiki/Beauty" title="Beauty">beauty</a>, virgin women and the <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a>. She was considered as the "Venus star" due to her similarities shared with the Roman goddess <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venus</a> as well as her luminosity equated to the <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">homologous planet</a>, which is, after the Sun and the Moon, the brightest celestial object in the night sky.</li>
<li><i>Chaupiñamca</i> is a divinity considered the feminine counterpart of the god Paryaqaqa. Like the latter, Chaupiñamca had five sisters, she being the eldest of all. She's represented as a rigid stone with five wings.</li>
<li><i>Chuychu</i> (<i>Rainbow</i>; a.k.a. <i>K'uychi</i>) is the beautiful <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbow</a> that was below both great gods (<a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Punchaw</a> and Ch'aska) and that was later elevated to the god of the nobles because it represented the beauty that was reserved for the nobles.</li>
<li><i>Coniraya</i> was the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> deity who fashioned his sperm into a fruit, which Cavillaca then ate.</li>
<li><i>Conopa</i> is a small, particularly shaped object worshiped at the domestic level in communities in the Andes of <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>.</li>
<li><i>Copacati</i> was a <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lake</a> goddess.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ekeko" title="Ekeko">Ekeko</a></i> was a god of the hearth and wealth. The ancients made dolls that represented him and placed a miniature version of their desires onto the doll; this was believed to cause the user to receive what he desired.</li>
<li><i>Huallallo Carhuincho</i> (<i>Yellowish mountain range</i>; a.k.a. <i>Huallallo Carhuancho, Wallallo Karwinchu</i>) was the god of <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fire</a> and the main god of the <a href="/wiki/Huanca" title="Huanca">wankas</a>, depicted as a human with <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dog</a> traits, with an evil profile and a devourer of children. Exiled to the jungles by Viracocha, he lives in solitude eating animals, although he also feeds on human flesh. One day, he meets a boy and plans to eat him. Then the boy revealing himself as <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>, the Sun god, is punished again by Viracocha and sent to an island, tied hand and foot, at the mercy of birds and other animals that will bite him for eternity.</li>
<li><i>Huamancantac</i> (A.k.a. <i>Guamancantac</i>) was the god of guano. Due to this, he's also known as the "Lord of guano". He's represented as an idol and was associated with guano birds. Coastal people made a lot of offerings to him with the purpose of extracting some of guano for agricultural and fishing issues.</li>
<li><i>Huari</i> (A.k.a. <i>Guari</i>) was the main god of <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín culture</a>. To them, Huari was the god of <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a> and was also associated with <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. Huari can turn into a <a href="/wiki/Puma_concolor" class="mw-redirect" title="Puma concolor">puma</a> or the proper lightning. It's believed that he had the main center of it in the alley of Conchucos, the same place where <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_de_Hu%C3%A1ntar" title="Chavín de Huántar">Chavín de Huántar</a> was erected. The Huari ethnic and possibly also the <a href="/wiki/Wari_culture" title="Wari culture">Wari culture</a> would continue the worship of this god under the same name, but with proper characteristics. To them, Huari was the giant god of war and was associated with the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, the water and agriculture. In addition to his giant aspect, Huari also can turn into a man, a snake and wind. Later, the incas would adopt him into their pantheon as well.</li>
<li><i>Huaytapallana</i> (<i>Place where flowers are collected</i>) was a god that had an important role during <a href="/wiki/Dry_season" title="Dry season">dry seasons</a>. In other legends, Huaytapallana was a woman with a captivating beauty, she was the daughter of the god Huallallo Carhuincho, Huaytapallana fell in love with Amaru, the son of Paryaqaqa. Paryaqaqa and Huallallo had a mutual enmity even before this event, enmity that led to Huallallo killing Amaru. Paryaqaqa saddened by the loss of his son, sent a powerful <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">flood</a> that drawned Huaytapallana. After that, both gods had a fierce battle that destroyed everything in their path, after the battle was over, Paryaqaqa was victorious, Huallallo angered turned into a devourer of humans, blaming them by his disgrace. <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a> watching these atrocities, punished Paryaqaqa and Huallallo for their cruelties turning both gods into snow mountains.</li>
<li><i>Hurkaway</i> was a guardian snake that lurks around in <a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">Uku Pacha</a>. It's believed that this creature is actually Urcaguary, the inca deity of metals and treasures.</li>
<li><i>Illapa</i> (<i>Thunder and lightning</i>; a.k.a. <i>Apu Illapa, Ilyap'a, Chuquiylla, Catuilla, Intillapa, Libiac</i>) was the god of <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>. In a general way, Illapa was the lord of the weather. Despite the fact that the main faculty of the deity was lightning and its other elements, Illapa had the absolute control of <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a>. Due to his faculty as weather god, Illapa was highly revered, especially in times of <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>. Illapa, as the god of war, played an essential role in war contexts. Illapa was the protective numen of the Inca military campaigns. These were quite frequent during the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Tahuantinsuyo</a>. As a result of his aforementioned powers, Illapa was considered the third most important god within the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Inca_Empire" title="Religion in the Inca Empire">Inca pantheon</a>. Only surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Wiracocha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>. He is represented as an imposing man in brilliant garments of gold and precious stones who lived in the <a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">upper world</a>. Likewise, Illapa carried a <a href="/wiki/Sling_(weapon)" title="Sling (weapon)">warak'a</a> with which he produced storms and a golden <a href="/wiki/Mace_(bludgeon)" title="Mace (bludgeon)">makana</a>, which symbolizes his power and the trinity of lightning bolt, thunder and lightning. According to the chronicler <a href="/wiki/Bernab%C3%A9_Cobo" title="Bernabé Cobo">Bernabé Cobo</a>, another representation that the Incas gave to Illapa was that of a warrior formed by <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">celestial world</a>. His rites took place in the highest <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a>, because they believed that Illapa lived in them. His rites consisted of dances, chants, festivals and <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">animal sacrifices</a> (in periods of great need, <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human offerings</a> were also made). Illapa manifested itself in the earthly world in the form of a puma or hawk. There is a legend that said that Illapa kept <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> that she drew from the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> in an urpu and gave it to her sister, <a href="/wiki/Mama_Killa" title="Mama Killa"> Mama Quilla</a>, to take care of her. When said urpu was filled, Illapa would throw a projectile from his huaraca to the urpu producing a roar that would cause thunder, the lightning would come to be the sparks produced by the impact and finally the water would come out as rain. It is said that the Incas, to attract the attention of the god so that he would produce rain, tied up black <a href="/wiki/Canis_familiaris" class="mw-redirect" title="Canis familiaris">dogs</a> and left them without food or drink. A time would pass in which these animals would begin to sob in pain from hunger and thirst. This made the god Illapa take pity on them and send rain to prevent their deaths. It is also mentioned that if the dogs were to die, this deity demonstrated his wrath by sending a powerful lightning bolt that would strike down without leaving a trace of those responsible for the death of said animals. Illapa took place in the <a href="/wiki/Coricancha" title="Coricancha">Coricancha</a> as well as the god Inti and other additional gods. The church of San Blas (Cuzco) was built on a temple where this god was worshiped. It is believed that <a href="/wiki/Sacsayhuam%C3%A1n" title="Sacsayhuamán">Sacsayhuamán</a> would have been used both as a military fortress and as a ceremonial temple, dedicated to various divinities, among which Illapa stood out. According to the chronicler <a href="/wiki/Crist%C3%B3bal_de_Molina" title="Cristóbal de Molina">Cristóbal de Molina</a>, Illapa had its own temple, which was known as Pucamarca.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a></i> (<i>Sun</i>; a.k.a. <i>Apu Inti, Apu Punchaw, Punchaw</i>) was the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a> god. Source of warmth and light and a protector of the people. Inti was considered the most important god. The Inca Emperors were believed to be the lineal descendants of the sun god.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ka-ata-killa" class="mw-redirect" title="Ka-ata-killa">Ka-ata-killa</a></i> was a pre-inca <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> goddess that was worshiped near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a>.</li>
<li><i>Kolash</i> (<i>Human from the nest</i>) was the god of <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">trills</a>. Kolash was born as a bird and later became a human, similar to god Paryaqaqa. Kolash expresses the essence of all things.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kon_(Inca_mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kon (Inca mythology)">Kon</a></i> (A.k.a. <i>Wakon</i>) was the god of <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> that came from the south. He was a son of Inti and Mama Killa. Kon was known as "The boneless god" because he was light weight, since he lacked bones and meat, despite this, he had a human form. Kon was also represented as a being with a felinic face, although it's believed that he wore feline masks, due to these characteristics, this god is known as "The flying feline", he carried trophy heads and a staff. In some <a href="/wiki/Huaco_(pottery)" title="Huaco (pottery)">huacos</a> he's also depicted as a man with bird traits or a feline with prominent eyes, due to his prominent eyes, he is also known as "The eyed god". Kon can also transform into a <a href="/wiki/Sandstorm" class="mw-redirect" title="Sandstorm">sandstorm</a> to move across the vast Peruvian coastal deserts. Kon created the first generation of humans, until his defeat and exile by Pachakamaq.</li>
<li><i>Mallko</i> was known as the first son of the sun god (Wiracocha or Inti) in Vichama's myth. This established Mallko as the brother of Vichama and half-brother of <a href="/wiki/Pacha_Kamaq" title="Pacha Kamaq">Pachakamaq</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kon_(pre-incan_mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kon (pre-incan mythology) (page does not exist)">Kon</a>. When Pachacámac tears his body to pieces to create food, from the remains of Mallko's navel and umbilical cord, the sun god creates another new child. This new child would be known as Vichama. In other representations, Mallko was the Inca god of law.</li>
<li><i>Mallku</i> (<i>Spirits of the mountains</i>) was a deity that represents the spirit and strength of the mountains. It takes the form of a powerful <a href="/wiki/Andean_condor" title="Andean condor">Condor</a>.</li>
<li><i>Mama Allpa</i> (<i>Mother Earth</i>) was a <a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">fertility</a> goddess depicted with multiple breasts.</li>
<li><i>Mama Koka</i> (<i>Mother of coca leaves</i>) was the goddess of <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a> and <a href="/wiki/Happiness" title="Happiness">happiness</a> in Inca mythology. She was originally a promiscuous woman who was torn in half by several jealous lovers. After her death, one of her parts would originate the <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a> plant, widely consumed by the Andean people, according to their mentality, these plants gave health and happiness.</li>
<li><i>Mama Nina</i> (<i>Mother of fire</i>) was the goddess of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>.</li>
<li><i>Mama Quinoa</i> (<i>Mother of quinoa grain</i>; a.k.a. <i>Quinoa mama, Quinua mama</i>) was the goddess of <a href="/wiki/Quinoa" title="Quinoa">quinoa</a> grain. Incas worshiped her fervently at the beginning of each planting season. The quinoa grain, or quinoa, was one of their main crops, and for 6,000 years it has been the staple food of the inhabitants of the Andes.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mama_Qucha" title="Mama Qucha">Mama Qucha</a></i> (<i>Sea mother</i>; a.k.a. <i>Mama Qocha, Mama Cocha</i>) was the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> goddess, protectress of sailors and fishermen. In one legend she mothered <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a> and Mama Killa with <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Wiraqucha</a>. Mama Qucha is considered one of the four elemental mothers, including Mama Nina (Mother of Fire), Pachamama (Mother Earth) and Mama Wayra (Mother of the Winds). Having as a curious fact that she, together with Pachamama and Mama Killa, form the three phases of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.</li></ul>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Santa_Cruz_Pachacuti_Yamqui_es.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Santa_Cruz_Pachacuti_Yamqui_es.gif/220px-Santa_Cruz_Pachacuti_Yamqui_es.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="241" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Santa_Cruz_Pachacuti_Yamqui_es.gif/330px-Santa_Cruz_Pachacuti_Yamqui_es.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Santa_Cruz_Pachacuti_Yamqui_es.gif/440px-Santa_Cruz_Pachacuti_Yamqui_es.gif 2x" data-file-width="2045" data-file-height="2240" /></a><figcaption>Representation of the cosmology of the Incas, according to Juan de Santa Cruz Pachacuti Yamqui Salcamayhua (1613), after a picture in the Sun Temple <a href="/wiki/Qurikancha" class="mw-redirect" title="Qurikancha">Qurikancha</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a> (the Sun), <a href="/wiki/Mama_Killa" title="Mama Killa">Mama Killa</a> (the Moon), Illapa (the Lightning), <a href="/wiki/Pachamama" title="Pachamama">Pachamama</a> (Mother Earth), Mama Qucha (Mother Sea), and <a href="/wiki/Chakana" title="Chakana">Chakana</a> (Southern Cross) with Saramama (Mother Corn) and Kukamama (Mother Coca).</figcaption></figure>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Mama_Pacha" class="mw-redirect" title="Mama Pacha">Mama Pacha</a></i> (<i>Mother nature</i> or <i>Mother Earth</i>; a.k.a. <i>Pachamama</i>) was considered a sacred being by the andean cosmovision, the mother of the hills and men since she not only cares for the material but also for the spiritual, protectress of nature, provider of water and food, favoring the fertility of the earth and sheltering human beings in exchange for help and protection, the Incas promoted the greatest veneration towards her and therefore her cult was important, because the success of the empire's <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvests</a> depended on it. Although it is considered a kind spirit and collaborator of human activities, it can also be hostile to those who do not respect nature; their rancor is shown through droughts, earthquakes, or making the weather unsuitable for the growth of food. She was the wife of Pachakamaq, who was considered the god of the <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a>, although in other legends, Pachakamaq was the god of <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fire</a> or earthquakes. The union of the Pachamama with Pachakamaq would come to represent the union of the earth and sky, from this union, <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mama_Killa" title="Mama Killa">Mama Killa</a> would be born and both were known as "The Willkas" that means "The sacred ones". Her artistic representation shows her as an adult woman who carries the harvest of <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca leaves</a>, another representation of this goddess is that of a <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragon</a>, she is also symbolized with a spiral.</li>
<li><i>Mama Rayhuana</i> was the goddess of flora and fauna, a source of energy and fecundity, fertility, under whose protection were vast cultivated territories of <a href="/wiki/Solanum_tuberosum" class="mw-redirect" title="Solanum tuberosum">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ullucus" title="Ullucus">ollucos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tropaeolum_tuberosum" title="Tropaeolum tuberosum">mashua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quinoa" title="Quinoa">quinoa</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mama_Quilla" class="mw-redirect" title="Mama Quilla">Mama Killa</a></i> (<i>Mother moon</i>) was a marriage, festival and <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> goddess and daughter of <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Wiraqucha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mama_Qucha" title="Mama Qucha">Mama Qucha</a>, as well as wife and sister of <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>. In one legend, she was the mother of <a href="/wiki/Manco_C%C3%A1pac" title="Manco Cápac">Manqu Qhapaq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacha_Kamaq" title="Pacha Kamaq">Pacha Kamaq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kon_(Pre-Incan_mythology)" title="Kon (Pre-Incan mythology)">Kon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mama_Ocllo" title="Mama Ocllo">Mama Uqllu</a>.</li>
<li><i>Mama Sara</i> (<i>Maize mother</i>; a.k.a. <i>Saramama, Zaramama</i>) was the goddess of <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a>. She was associated with <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> that grew in multiples or were similarly strange. These strange plants were sometimes dressed as dolls of Mama Sara. She was also associated with willow trees. She had several subjects:
<ul><li><i>Kuka Manka</i> (<i>Coca cup constellations</i>) was a constellation that took care of magical herbs.</li>
<li><i>Sara Manka</i> (<i>Corn cup constellation</i>) a constellation that took care of plant foods.</li></ul></li>
<li><i>Mama Wayra</i> (<i>Mother of wind</i>) was the goddess of air and <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">winds</a>, protectress of the <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>. She was considered as a purifying goddess.</li>
<li><i>Manañamca</i> was a malevolent femenine deity, partner of the god Huallallo Carhuincho. Like the latter, she faced the god Paryaqaqa but he defeated her and threw her into the sea.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pacha_Kamaq" title="Pacha Kamaq">Pacha Kamaq</a></i> (<i>The Soul of Earth, The Earth maker, The Earth shaker</i>; a.k.a. <i>Pachacámac, Pachakamaq</i>) was a chthonic creator god, reissue of god <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a> and also a god associated with the <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a>. It's said the ancient Peruvians thought that a single movement of his head would cause massive cataclysms, and if he'd move completely, the world will come to its end, since Pachakamaq was a god associated with being able to predict the future and control the movements of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">earth</a>, due to this, Pachakamaq is also known as the "God of the earthquakes". Far from being the one who protects people from telluric movements, he was the one who provoked them and who had to be pleased and offered so that he would not send said scourge. Pachakamaq was represented on a long wooden <a href="/wiki/Cult_image" title="Cult image">idol</a>. The bottom part of the idol shows zoomorphic, ornitomorphic and anthropomorphic designs along with <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">crops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_optics" title="Atmospheric optics">atmospheric phenomena</a>. It's believed that part represents the earth's creation by Pachakamaq. The upper part of the idol shows Pachakamaq with two faces of aggressive expression, representing a symbol of the duality of pre-hispanic andean thought. He was considered as the creator god of the second generation of humans, after a fierce and long battle between Pachakamaq and the god <a href="/wiki/Kon_(Pre-Incan_mythology)" title="Kon (Pre-Incan mythology)">Kon</a>, a battle in which Kon would end up being defeated and banished by the victorious Pachakamaq. Pachakamaq was worshiped earlier by the <a href="/wiki/Ichma" class="mw-redirect" title="Ichma">Ichma</a> and was highly respected, because no one could not look him directly eye to eye and even his priests entered backwards to see him. Due to his powerful influence, the Incas adopted him into their pantheon as part of the Inca creation myth. Only high dignitaries entered the great temples, but ordinary pilgrims could observe and make their own sacrifices in the plazas.</li>
<li><i>Paryaqaqa</i> (<i>Stone falcon</i>) was the god of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> in pre-inca mythology, coming from an ethnic group called Yauyos and later adopted by the <a href="/wiki/Huanca" title="Huanca">Huanca culture</a> when the Huancas were defeated by the Yauyos, when the Incas defeated both ethnic groups, Paryaqaqa was later adopted by them into their pantheon as well. He was a <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storm</a> god and considered as a creator god. He was born as a <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcon</a> like his five brothers in Condorcoto mountain, to later become a Kolash (human from the nest). One legend says this god left his divine life in the <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a> when he saw a humble man crying, Paryaqaqa dismayed, asked him the reason for his sadness, he told the god that another god called Huallallo Carhuincho threatened the people of the saddened man that if they don't give him enough human sacrifices, he'd burn the whole village, so Paryaqaqa deciced, went to fight against the malevolent god, when the battle was over, the god Paryaqaqa was victorious and then the village was set free from the tyranny of Huallallo, the people of the village worshiped Paryaqaqa fervently as a sign of gratefulness. It's believed Paryaqaqa, along with Catequil, were different representations of god Illapa due to the three gods share strong similarities like the absolute control of atmospheric phenomena and they present warrior and dominant facets in their respective legends.</li>
<li><i>Paricia</i> was a god who sent a flood to kill humans who did not respect him adequately. Possibly another name for Paryaqaqa.</li>
<li><i>Piguerao</i> (A.k.a. <i>Pikiru</i>) was the tutelar god of <a href="/wiki/Night" title="Night">night</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evil" title="Evil">evil</a>.</li>
<li><i>Puñuy</i> (A.k.a. Puñui) was a divinity associated with <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dreams</a> and the act of <a href="/wiki/Sleeping" class="mw-redirect" title="Sleeping">sleeping</a>. There was a sanctuary dedicated to this deity in Cusco. In it, rituals were executed with the purpose of obtaining a good sleep and not dying while sleeping.</li>
<li><i>Qhaxra-kamayuq</i> (<i>The one who takes care of sowing</i>) was a guardian deity who made an effort to prevent thieves from entering houses.</li>
<li><i>Qhoa</i> (A.k.a. <i>Qoa, Coa, Ccoa, K'oa, Khoa, Cahua, Caua, Qowa, Quwa</i>) was a big feline deity that lives in <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a> and was able to bring the <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storms</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>. Qhoa was depicted as a large winged flying feline (that can be a <a href="/wiki/Puma_concolor" class="mw-redirect" title="Puma concolor">puma</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Panthera_onca" class="mw-redirect" title="Panthera onca">jaguar</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Andean_mountain_cat" title="Andean mountain cat">oscollo</a>). As a deity considered to be the bringer of rain and storms that help the growth of <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">crops</a> and fertilization of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">earth</a> (similar to god Illapa), Incas worshiped it fervently. The Qhoa was a benevolent deity to whom they asked for rain and it was granted. But like all sacred beings, Qhoa also launched its punishments through hail or storms. It's believed this deity jumped from <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> to cloud, dropping <a href="/wiki/Thunderbolt" title="Thunderbolt">lightning bolts</a> that come out of its eyes to the earth, its loud roar was the <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a>, its urine was the rain and the flapping of its large wings would be hail. In some andean communities they still say that Qhoa plays in the heights, entering and leaving the <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoons</a>.</li>
<li><i>Qoyllur</i> (<i>Star</i>; a.k.a. <i>Coyllur, Quyllur</i>) was the goddess of the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, as well as Qoyllur had an important position as a deity of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>. She was the companion of <a href="/wiki/Mama_Killa" title="Mama Killa">Mama Killa</a>, they were always together since without Qoyllur's company the nights would not have the same luminosity, the stars were a perfect complement.</li>
<li><i>Rímac and Chaclla</i> were two brother gods who sacrificed themselves to end a drought that plagued the coast in ancient times. Rímac became a river and Chaclla became the rain.</li>
<li><i>Runacoto</i> was a divinity associated with masculine virility. Due to this, people with a short virile member went to ask him for help to grow their virile member.</li>
<li><i>Sorimana</i> (A.k.a. <i>Solimana</i>) was a pre-inca god of volcanoes and earthquakes. Solimana also shares the name of a <a href="/wiki/Solimana_(volcano)" title="Solimana (volcano)">volcano</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Arequipa" title="Arequipa">Arequipa</a>, Peru.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Supay" title="Supay">Supay</a></i> was both the god of <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a> and ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Uku_Pacha" class="mw-redirect" title="Uku Pacha">Uku Pacha</a> as well as a race of <a href="/wiki/Demon" title="Demon">demons</a>. Supay was also the personification of all evilness. However, he was considered an ambivalent god that could be considered both <a href="/wiki/Evil" title="Evil">evil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Good" title="Good">good</a>. Supay was represented as a human figure with a strong resemblance to demons, having long <a href="/wiki/Horn_(anatomy)" title="Horn (anatomy)">horns</a> and ears, a felinic head, very perceptive eyes and sharp teeth. Supay also has the ability to turn into a beautiful Inca woman as well as a very attractive Inca man, these abilities turns him into a danger for those who did not show respect or who tried to make fun of him. Despite this, there were people who believed in his great power, thus reaching the point of worshiping him, so that he would grant them favors for evil or good through <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rituals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">offerings</a> and the creation of <a href="/wiki/Altar" title="Altar">altars</a>. The ancient legends told that Supay went beyond that evil that he evoked, since they described him as the protector of the path that will be traveled when dying, likewise, the Incas believed that after <a href="/wiki/Dying" title="Dying">dying</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> passes into the background. This background for them meant a new beginning with the Inca gods. Despite the description of the Supay as an evil being, he was good at the end of days for those who awaited his death, which made the Incas believe that since ancient times, the god Supay was a being that equilibrated the balance between the good and evil. In addition to giving them the feeling that there will always be evil, but not enough.</li>
<li><i>Temenduare and Arikute</i> were brother gods who, with their clashes, caused a flood. This as a result of conjuring a bestial being provided with a hundred legs of water. Temenduare and Arikute are thought to be other names for the gods Vichama and Mallko.</li>
<li><i>Tumayricapac and Tumayhanampa</i> were pre-inca twin gods from a region called Chinchaycocha. These two are associated with the <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> and are considered as civilizing heroes as well.</li>
<li><i>Tunupa</i> was the aymara god of volcanoes as well as lightning and water. According to Collasuyo's myths, Tunupa put order in the world and is often confused with <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a>.</li>
<li><i>Tulumanya</i> (A.k.a. <i>Turumanyay</i>) was the first <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbow</a> (rainbow of the ancients), from whose chest the <a href="/wiki/Amaru_(mythology)" title="Amaru (mythology)">Amaru</a> is born by Viracocha's orders.</li>
<li><i>Urcaguary</i> was the inca deity of <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">jewels</a> and other underground items of great value. The gender of this deity is ambiguous, so it can be a feminine or masculine divinity. Urcaguary lives beneath mountains protecting <a href="/wiki/Treasure" title="Treasure">treasures</a> and jewels from evil people who dare to steal them. Urcaguary was represented with a snake body and a <a href="/wiki/Taruca" title="Taruca">taruka</a> head, gold chains and precious stones were linked in its snake tail. It's believed that the taruka or deer head is due to its way of thinking.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Urcuchillay" title="Urcuchillay">Urquchillay</a></i> was the god of <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">cattle</a> and domestic animals. Urquchillay was worshipped mainly by Inca herders because he watched over animals and maintained the welfare of the herds and multiply their offspring. Urquchillay was represented as a strong man with a <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> head as well as a multicoloured llama or ram.</li>
<li><i>Urpihuachay</i> (<i>The one that gives birth to pigeons</i>; a.k.a. <i>Urpihuachac, Urpayhuachac, Urpayhuachay</i>) was the creator goddess of the <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fishes</a>, coming originally from <a href="/wiki/Chincha_culture" title="Chincha culture">Chincha culture</a> and later adopted by the Inca pantheon as wife of Pachakamaq in some legends. She's depicted as a woman with mixed fish and bird traits. In one legend, it's said after Cavillace and her son jumped into the sea and turned into two isles in front of the sanctuary of the god Pachakamaq, Coniraya decided to get revenge against Pachakamaq and sought Urpihuachay and her daughters with the attempt to rape them, the goddess wasn't there, she was visiting Cavillace already turned into an island, Coniraya took advantage and found the goddess daughters, fortunately, they could escape from the angered god by turning themselves into birds and flying far away. Coniraya still angered, threw all Urpihuachay's belongings to the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, among them, the fishes only Urpihuachay knew how to raise, these were thrown in <a href="/wiki/Pond" title="Pond">ponds</a> near the temple, once in the sea, fishes multiplied since then and Urpihuachay was considered as the mother of all birds and fishes.</li>
<li><i>Vichama</i> (A.k.a. <i>Wichama, Atipa</i>) was the son of the sun god (Wiracocha or Inti) in Vichama's myth. This established Vichama as the brother of Mallko and the half-brother of <a href="/wiki/Pacha_Kamaq" title="Pacha Kamaq">Pachakamaq</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kon_(pre-incan_mythology)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kon (pre-incan mythology) (page does not exist)">Kon</a>. He was created by the sun god from the remains of his brother Mallko's umbilical cord and navel, which was torn to pieces by Pachakamaq's wrath. In the aforementioned myth, Vichama was a divinity associated with <a href="/wiki/Revenge" title="Revenge">revenge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a> and, to a lesser extent, with <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a></i> (<i>Quechua: Apu Kon Illa Tiqsi Wiraqucha Pachayachachiq Pachakamaq; English: Great Lord, eternal light, source of life, knowledge and creator of the world</i>) was the god of everything. It's said he came from the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a> and created the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> to light up the world that was inmersed in <a href="/wiki/Darkness" title="Darkness">darkness</a>, Viracocha also creates the <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a> (ordering the sun to move itself in the <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a>). Following the creation, he created the <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humanity</a> by blowing on the <a href="/wiki/Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone">stones</a>, but he didn't like what he created because from this first attempt to create humanity, they turned out to be strong and violent <a href="/wiki/Giant" title="Giant">giants</a> with no intelligence (in other legends, the first attempt was conceived before the creation of the sun and humanity turned out to be Ñawpa Machus that means "the primordial old ones" {{Citation needed|date=March 2023}} , depicted as tall and skinny humans, despite this fact, they had a brutal strength and were as violent as evil, then Viracocha created the sun, irradiating the light that put an end to them), the giants didn't recognize Viracocha as their creator and they rebelled against him, then Viracocha sent a <a href="/wiki/Unu_Pachakuti" title="Unu Pachakuti">devastating deluge</a> that destroyed them, and from the remaining small stones, he created a better humanity. Viracocha had a lot of representations around the civilizations and cultures that worshiped him, the most known is that of a sun crown man or anthropomorphic man with two staffs or <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning bolts</a> in his hands on a platform. Viracocha had his face with tears in the form of <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>. It's said he wept when he saw the suffering of the creatures he had created. Viracocha was also associated with the <a href="/wiki/Cougar" title="Cougar">puma</a>. Another representation of him was a stone with egg's shape that is considered as a <a href="/wiki/World_egg" class="mw-redirect" title="World egg">cosmic egg</a>. Viracocha was present as the creator of everything in existence in several ancient civilizations and cultures around <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> like <a href="/wiki/Casma-Sechin_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Casma-Sechin culture">Sechin culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caral-Supe_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Caral-Supe civilization">Caral-Supe civilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huari_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Huari culture">Wari culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiwanaku" title="Tiwanaku">Tiwanaku</a>, etc. Incas weren't the exception, they considered Viracocha as the creator of all the Cosmos as well as the substance that gives rise to all of things. In the beginning he was the main god, but when Pachakuti became Inca emperor, he changed this god's importance, pointing out that the most important god was Inti, this is because the support of Inti against the <a href="/wiki/Chanka" title="Chanka">Chankas</a>, turning the Inca <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Cusco" title="Kingdom of Cusco">Kingdom of Cusco</a> into a great and prosperous empire. Despite this fact, Viracocha was still worshiped fervently, but just the <a href="/wiki/Sapa_Inca" title="Sapa Inca">Sapa Incas</a> or emperors were allowed to worship him, leaving Inti as the main god of the Inca people.</li>
<li><i>Wasikamayuq</i> (<i>The one who takes care of home</i>) was the tutelary god of home. Wasikamayuq was supported by other deities like Qhaxra-kamayuq, they both ensured security in Inca homes.</li>
<li><i>Yanañamca and Tutañamca</i> (<i>Huaca of the darkness</i> and <i>Huaca of the night</i>) were the twin gods of <a href="/wiki/Darkness" title="Darkness">darkness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Night" title="Night">night</a>. They ruled the world at the beginning of time, before the gods took care of the earth. Viracocha sends Huallallo Carhuincho, god of fire, to defeat them and, at the same time, illuminate the earth, although the latter stayed taking advantage of it and devouring his faithful ones.</li>
<li><i>Yana Raman</i> (A.k.a. <i>Libiac Cancharco, Libiac</i>) was the pre-inca god of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>. He's considered as the main god and hence the creator of an ethnic group called Yaros or Llacuaces. Likewise, he's considered as the base of the cult of god Illapa, that means, when the Incas assimilated Yaros within <a href="/wiki/Tahuantinsuyo" class="mw-redirect" title="Tahuantinsuyo">Tahuantinsuyo</a>, the god Yana Raman was renewed as the god Illapa.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Important_beliefs">Important beliefs</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Important beliefs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1251242444"><table class="box-Prose plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is in <a href="/wiki/MOS:LIST" class="mw-redirect" title="MOS:LIST">list</a> format but may read better as <a href="/wiki/MOS:PROSE" class="mw-redirect" title="MOS:PROSE">prose</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit">converting this section</a>, if appropriate. <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">Editing help</a> is available.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2024</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1251242444"><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Inca_mythology" title="Special:EditPage/Inca mythology">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22">"Inca mythology"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Inca+mythology%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Inca+mythology%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Inca+mythology%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2024</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mama_Ocllo" title="Mama Ocllo">Mama Uqllu</a> was the sister and wife of <a href="/wiki/Manco_C%C3%A1pac" title="Manco Cápac">Manqu Qhapaq</a>. She was thought to have taught the Inca the art of spinning.</li>
<li><i>Mamaconas</i> were similar to nuns and lived in temple sanctuaries.  They dedicated their lives to <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>, and served the Inca and priests. Young girls of the nobility or of exceptional beauty were trained for four years as <i>acllas</i> and then had the option of becoming mamaconas or marrying Inca nobles. They are comparable to the Roman <a href="/wiki/Vestal_Virgins" class="mw-redirect" title="Vestal Virgins">Vestal Virgins</a>, though Inca society did not value virginity as a virtue the way Western societies have done throughout history.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>In one legend, <i><a href="/wiki/Unu_Pachakuti" title="Unu Pachakuti">Unu Pachakuti</a></i> was a great flood sent by Virachocha to destroy the giants that built <a href="/wiki/Tiwanaku" title="Tiwanaku">Tiwanaku</a>.</li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Huaca" title="Huaca">Wak'a</a> was a sacred object such as a mountain or a mummy.</li>
<li>Ancestor worship has been a staple of Andean society before, during, and after the Inca Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The traditional communities of the Andes are known as <a href="/wiki/Ayllu" title="Ayllu">ayllu</a> which are familial clans that trace their origins to a common ancestor. A form of ancestor worship practiced by the Inca was the <a href="/wiki/Mummification" class="mw-redirect" title="Mummification">mummification</a> and respect for their deceased relatives' remains. These mummies would be provided food, drink, clothing, and valuable items, they were considered links to the family and the gods and were consulted when the family needed spiritual advice.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Panaqa" class="mw-redirect" title="Panaqa">panaqa</a> was a family formed by all of the descendants of the king, <a href="/wiki/Sapa_Inka" class="mw-redirect" title="Sapa Inka">Sapa Inka</a>  with only the son and heir being excluded from this family. The reason for this is so the Auqui, <a href="/wiki/Crown_prince" title="Crown prince">crown prince</a>, forms his own panaqa. One of the major functions of the panaqa was to maintain the mummy and the memory of deceased Sapa Inca's.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Important_places">Important places</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inca_mythology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Important places"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Inca cosmology was ordered in three spatio-temporal levels or <i><a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)#Ukhu_Pacha" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">Pachas</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heydt-Coca_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heydt-Coca-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These included: <a href="/wiki/Uku_Pacha" class="mw-redirect" title="Uku Pacha">Uku Pacha</a> ("the lower world"), which was located within the earth's surface; <a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)#Kay_Pacha" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">Kay Pacha</a>, which was the material world;  and <a href="/wiki/Pacha_(Inca_mythology)#Hanan_Pacha" title="Pacha (Inca mythology)">Hanan Pacha</a> ("higher world"), which was the world above us where the sun and moon lived.<sup id="cite_ref-Steele2004_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steele2004-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The environment and geography were integral part of Inca mythology as well. Many prominent natural features within the Inca Empire were tied to important myths and legends amongst the Inca.<sup id="cite_ref-Toohey_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toohey-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a>, an important body of water on the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano</a>, was incorporated into Inca myths, as the lake of origins from which the world began.<sup id="cite_ref-Toohey_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toohey-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similarly, many of prominent Andean peaks played special roles within the mythology of the Incas. This is reflected in myths about the <a href="/wiki/Paxil" class="mw-redirect" title="Paxil">Paxil</a> mountain, from which people were alleged to have been created from <a href="/wiki/Corn_kernels" class="mw-redirect" title="Corn kernels">corn kernels</a> that were scattered by the gods.<sup id="cite_ref-Toohey_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toohey-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Terrestrial environments were not the only type of environment that was important to mythology. The Incas often incorporated the <a href="/wiki/Stars" class="mw-redirect" title="Stars">stars</a> into legends and myths.<sup id="cite_ref-Penprase_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penprase-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, many <a href="/wiki/Constellations" class="mw-redirect" title="Constellations">constellations</a> were given names and were incorporated into stories, such as the star formations of the Great Llama and the Fox.<sup id="cite_ref-Penprase_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penprase-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While perhaps not relating to a single physical feature per se, environmental <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> was extremely important in Incan mythology. For example, in the creation myth of <a href="/wiki/Viracocha" title="Viracocha">Viracocha</a> the sound of the god's voice is particularly important. Additionally, myths were transmitted orally, so the acoustics and sound of a location were important for Incan mythology.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These examples demonstrate the power that environment held in creating and experiencing Incan myths.
</p><p>The most important temple in the Inca Empire was known as <a href="/wiki/Coricancha" title="Coricancha">Coricancha</a> ("The Golden Temple" in <a href="/wiki/Quechuan_languages" title="Quechuan languages">Quechua</a>) which was located in the heart of Incan <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a> and according to Inca legend was built by <a href="/wiki/Manco_C%C3%A1pac" title="Manco Cápac">Manco Cápac</a> as a place of worship for the principle deity of the Inca, the sun god <a href="/wiki/Inti" title="Inti">Inti</a>. During the reign of Pachakutiq Inca this temple was the home of the riches of the Inca Empire, housing gold, important religious artifacts, and gilded effigies of important Incan deities.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Coricancha being in the heart of Cusco, which is in the heart of the Inca Empire, is the point of convergence of the 41 pathways leading out of Cusco into the rest of the empire with a system called ceque, which served a political, religious, and administrative role in the Inca Empire. The Coricancha was the site of important religious ceremonies, such as during the <a href="/wiki/Inti_Raymi" title="Inti Raymi">Inti Raymi</a> in which after a procession through Cusco, the <a href="/wiki/Sapa_Inka" class="mw-redirect" title="Sapa Inka">Sapa Inka</a> would enter the Coricancha.
